On 3/24/2012 4:27 PM, Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> (snip)
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> for.

I'm not sure if this qualifies, but I have written CICS COBOL programs 
using sockets for TCP/IP communications.  I cannot claim I did the hard 
work, since I was copying from another programmer's example (also in 
COBOL).  We needed one assembler subprogram for posting an ECB, in order 
to switch between send and receive.  The real work was done by calling 
subprograms with names like "EZASOKET".

A couple of things stand out in my mind.  Unlike coding for BTAM or 
VTAM, where a communications line is unidirectional, a TCP/IP socket is 
truly bidirectional, so switching between sending and receiving was very 
important.  Another thing that seemed truly odd to me was that we needed 
to receive the first two bytes of a message to get the length of the 
data, and do another CALL to receive the data.  Then if we didn't 
receive all the data in that CALL, we had to loop back and do another 
CALL to get more data.  The same situation could occur on sending data. 
  If we couldn't get the whole message out in one CALL, we had to do 
another CALL starting with the rest of the message.

I have some familiarity with a couple of assembly languages, RPG, 
Easytrieve, BASIC, and C.  But COBOL is what I know best, and I would 
have had a more difficult time in any other language.  We had some 
teething problems with that conversion to TCP/IP, mostly in 
error-handling and automatically reconnecting if a socket failed.  It 
was an interesting project.

Kind regards,
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Interesting article

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-03-28T04:02:03+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9te33dF30tU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<N5adnarR0OXzK_rSnZ2dnUVZ_rCdnZ2d@westnet.com.au> <jk8g71$fot$2@reader1.panix.com> <9t1ldpF7oqU1@mid.individual.net> <9t1umeFr06U1@mid.individual.net> <9t6si2F9ktU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article <9t1umeFr06U1@mid.individual.net>,
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> writes:
…[61 more quoted lines elided]…
> a lot of the blame on academia.

It is certainly true that Acadaemia by-and-large decided COBOL wasn't worth 
pursuing long before industry did. And I suppose that makes them culpable to 
some extent. It depends on how you see the role of Acadaemia. If you see 
their role as being to provide what industry is looking for, then you have a 
valid argument that they didn't do that.

But I have friends who teach in Universities and Polytechnics and they would 
argue it is the role of Polytechnics to do that. The "Higher Institutions" 
should be more concerned with finding new and better ways to do things and 
providing those ways to industry. Their role (as these people see it) is to 
push the known boundaries and come up with new and innovative stuff, that 
may have no application at all in the real world. While that may sound 
idealistic and high-falutin', they soon start to realise that they won't get 
funding unless they provide something of some use to somebody :-)

I honestly don't think you can hold Acadaemia responsible because COBOL 
ceased to be the best way to do something. I think it became apparent to 
people doing research and using networks as the lifeblood of their work that 
objects and layers were going to be the way to go and so they focused 
attention on languages that did that rather than languages that didn't.

When OO COBOL first became available there were a handful of people in this 
group who thought it had a future and were prepared to use it.  The vast 
majority were fiercely against it.There were heated debates about it and how 
pointless and unnecessary it was and the fact that it was just modular 
programming re-invented (it isn't...). In fact, Bill, your own anti-OO post 
is very mild compared to some that were posted back then...:-)

I became tired of it and stopped defending it as it is a bit like trying to 
explain "red" to a hysterically blind person. It isn't that they can't see, 
it is just that they don't want to. I started to realise I was getting wound 
up about something that I really had no vested interest in so I went off and 
pursued my own investigations and trials with it and found it very useful, 
for a number of years. It was during that time that I realised the 
difference between component based and modular development, the real 
importance of separation and encapsulation, layering of software and why you 
should do it, polymorphism for reuse, and a number of other similar 
subtleties that can make life as a software developer so much easier. 
Eventually, I got pushed into dropping my beloved COBOL  and for the last 6 
years or so I have enjoyed pushing the C# envelope, but it was OO COBOL that 
got me the basic concepts and gave me the Keys to the Kingdom.

This weekend the local Polytechnic is holding a Mashup contest for students 
and I have been asked to attend as one of a number of mentors to assist the 
kids with their project planning, marketing planning, presentations, and 
programming. The "programming" is far removed from what most people here 
would call programming, and yet, it is just as productive as what we do with 
our languages. They will be writing Python, PHP, C#, Java, but mostly 
Javascript, XML and XAML and using these languages in a very basic way to 
provide "glue" for APIs that they can bring together into a single web 
application. These kids will be writing Web Services and SaaS applications 
that will do useful work and  they will do it in two days. The point I'm 
trying to make here is that the world really has moved on and it is silly to 
argue about whether (OO) COBOL has a place in it.

They take it in their stride to access and annotate Google Maps, pull 
together music tracks and videos from different web sites, insert GPS data 
and geolocations into their applications and they could end up with (just as 
one simple example) a web application that lets you take a mapped tour of 
where they live, with videos of points of interest along the way, while you 
listen to a selection of music you can choose and stop off at each of their 
Facebook friends houses as you go :-)  You can't do THAT in COBOL... :-) 
(Actually, you can if you use OO COBOL but it would be mind-numbingly 
tedious to write and you'd have to "translate" all the documented APIs into 
COBOL before you could even start. You'd be lucky to do it in a weekend.)

Reading the above you will probably say that they can't do an accounting 
system, or a detailed sales analysis or complex database accesses. Sorry, 
they can... Commercial applications can be mashed up just as easily as 
entertainment ones. In fact, it is likely that this weekend's winner will be 
a commercial business application.

I'm looking forward to it... :-)

It made me smile when I saw that each finished application will be 
transferred to a memory stick and given to the judges at a specified time on 
Sunday. (They also have to email milestone documents and copies of things 
like their Project Plan at certain times along the way.) Remember when 
application deployment was a team thing that took a full day? Now it's 
copying an XML file to a memory stick then plugging that in to an Internet 
connected USB port. :-)

This is the new generation I have mentioned in passing in a number of posts 
here. In a couple more years they will be working in industry. I don't see 
them going to IT and being told it will take 6 months to develop the 
application they want because the mainframe's programmed in COBOL and there 
are certain procedures and traditions that must be complied with... :-)



> A place I just spent 25 years in and
> had the chance to see first hand how they are not as interested in
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> the IT world this gives us OOP where everything is an object.

No, Bill, everything is a Class. The Class is represented by an object 
instance of itself.


>  Except
> that is not an accurate world view.

On the contrary, it is a very accurate view of the world. Ever since people 
could write they have been observing the world and classifying groups of 
things in it. Probably even before that things were classified as friendly, 
dangerous, edible, comforting, and so on. Without Classification the whole 
foundation of Natural Science would be disordered and chaotic. In fact it is 
hard to even imagine it without the familiar taxonomy we learn at school.



>And in the natural sciences we
> see the scientific method being warped to draw a conclussion and then
> prove it throwing away any data that doesn't fit your model.

I have never met a scientist who did that. I agree it has happened in some 
quite famous cases (where the perpetrators were exposed) but it isn't fair 
to say that that is the norm. You diminish dedicated people all over the 
world when you say that.


>And in
> the Business Schools, well, what do I have to say.  Look at the
> business world today and you can clearly see what they are getting in
> those MBA classes.

Apparently "they" are getting something you disapprove of?

And yet the world hasn't stopped.

>
>>                                        and, again, I'm on record as
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> Why?

Man, I really wish I had time to give this the answer it deserves... :-) 
Unfortunately, I'm already working on another book about something entirely 
different.

Some other time...

>
>>                            and  there are other tools that fit the OO
>> paradigm better than COBOL does.
>
> See my comment above.  The world is not all objects.

Maybe not, but it IS all Classes of things. (Including abstract things like 
ideas, thoughts, feelings, and emotions, as well as cats, dogs, biscuits, 
cars and cheeses...; all of these can be represented  by Classes. (Abstract 
Classes and normal Classes))


> The lisp guys
> couldn't make it so

I'm no LISP expert but I remember writing a couple of trial programs with it 
many years ago. I don't remember it referencing objects. Seem to recall it 
was more about processing lists of things in order to simulate 
intelligence...



>and OOP isn't going to do it either.

It HAS done it. It happened while you weren't looking... :-) (See above)



Eventually
> (and it appears sooner rather than later) academia will tire of it
> and develop a new paradigm.

I sure hope so. It is their job to improve things.



> And mosty will jump on that bandwagon,
> too.

If something is demonstrably better than what you have, how is it jumping on 
a bandwagon to embrace it? Wouldn't it be foolish NOT to?
>
>>                                  Mostly they are free and COBOL
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> academia, it is grossly overpriced.

Visual Studio is certainly NOT overpriced. (It is a superb product.) And the 
price is academic anyway because most people don't need the full version. I 
started off using the Express Edition with C# and found it so amazing I 
thought I'd buy the full product. It gave me no enhancements to C# other 
than I had already, but it did provide C++, VB.Net, phone development SDK, 
and a bunch of other things that I'll probably never use. It is still well 
worth the money. My point is that, at least as far as C# is concerned, there 
is no discernible difference between the Express Edition and the Full 
Edition. So you DO get the "bells and whistles" with the FREE version.

You are severely misinformed. The Express Edition of Visual Studio is 
completely FREE. (Just like the Express Edition of SQL Server.)  You will be 
required to register your use of the software but there is no charge and 
never has been. Furthermore, you can use the software for commercial 
development and distribute anything you build with these Editions, without 
paying any form of royalty or fee to Microsoft whatsoever or being 
constrined by the software being "nobbled".

As for "bells and whistles" you can only run SQL Server Express on ONE CPU, 
so if you want to start a server farm you probably will want to pay for the 
full version. However, most people, including businesses, are perfectly 
happy running it on a dedicated server. It networks over the LAN and the 
Internet extremely well and I regularly access remote and local instances of 
it (There... "instances", something that networked systems like to have...) 
I ran some performance comparisions yesterday between SQL Server and ORACLE. 
I'm not going into detail, but SQL server was around three times faster in 
most cases...It takes noticeably longer to connect, apart from anything 
else). I am NOT running either of them locally on a dedicated server; they 
run just as any other app on the machine. The same machine runs PostgreSQL, 
MySQL, MS Access 2003+, and SQL Lite when required to as well, although 
since I acquired SQL Server these have fallen into disuse and I retain them 
purely for testing the Toolset.)

COBOL is free? Care to tell Micro Focus, IBM, and Alchemy that? (The last 
quote I received for a five seat NON-CIL (native code) generating COBOL 
compiler, with support, and upgrades for a year: $US20,000. A "special 
price" because it was not their latest offering. It was this which led me to 
think about putting COBOL in the Cloud. (See "COBOL on Tap" thread in this 
forum) However, I'm gradually coming to the conclusion that I should just 
let COBOL go completely and rewrite the generators in the Toolset to produce 
C#. It is not as big a deal as I first thought.


>With free, you get what you pay
> for!!
>

Guess I've been lucky... I got enough to encourage me to support the 
products and buy the full versions where it made sense.

>>
>> but, much more importantly, they achieve more in less time and that
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> language.

There is nothing heroic in doing something that was attempted by someone 
patently unqualified to do it. Not that it matters, but I have seen people 
spend days trying to fix COBOL.
>
>>       It isn't the fault of COBOL. The world has simply moved on and
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> do with it?

You might be struggling with the next generation of multi-cored 
multi-processing systems that are just around the corner... COBOL doesn't 
support them; other languages do.


>  I am doing COBOL with networked database access.  I have
> done Web programming in COBOL. I have never had a reason to do it but
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> old".

So do I. It should be refactored properly, not because it is old, but 
because it is valuable.
>
>>
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> protect their jobs by writting obfuscated code.

Those sites are/were the minority. I have never worked with any COBOL 
programmer who was so insecure that they deliberately obfuscated code in the 
interests of job security. I have seen obfuscated code, certainly, but it 
was usually a result of over-maintenance, and the "English-like" nature of 
the language which encourages people to think in English instead of thinking 
in Boolean... :-)
>
>>
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>

The man was right. That does not reflect badly on him.

>>
>> Call yourself a COBOL Programmer? Don't bleat about it, fix it. :-)
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> it either!!  Am I the only one who sees the flaws in this?

You are the only one who sees no merit in it whatsoever. Why do you suppose 
they are doing this? Because they hate COBOL? Doubt it.

I have to say I was very sceptical about sites programmed in SQL. Then, I 
had to manage one and I found that the guys were expert at what they did and 
some of what they did was really elegant. They used visual toolsets to do 
jobs in minutes that would take a good COBOL guy a couple of days. They 
could visually drag and drop tables into joins and selects, sort stuff, 
merge and update  etc., all in minutes and without having to write any code. 
It was pretty impressive. And when they DID write code it was pretty amazing 
too. I found out that SQL can do a heap of stuff I never knew it could (my 
own SQL was greatly improved due to this experience and it was a good 
grounding for when I came to write the PRIMA Toolset.)

There is a very good case for them training people in SQL, using it to 
manipulate their data resources and then calling off reports in Crystal. 
They are not using indexed files any more so they don't need a COBOL program 
every time they want to know something. Crystal is another visual tool which 
non-IT users can learn fairly quickly. And their data is now open and 
accessible by all kinds of third party tools used by anyone who has the 
necessary permissions. People (with no COBOL Knowledge) can attach Excel and 
Access to the main database and slice and dice the stuff they want.  You see 
no advantages in any of this?

>
>>
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> oriented. Primarily read records, tally sums, print reports.

Which is EXACTLY why Crystal was invented... :-)

>
>>
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> tool."

I once played a round of golf with a guy who had a low single digit 
handicap. Just for a laugh he played every shot with a putter. He explained 
to me that everything is about timing and showed how he could make a putter 
work like a wood by shortening his grip and changing the timing of his 
swing.

It was fascinating and I have never forgotten it. I haven't played golf for 
a long time but I'll never forget that particular morning on a public course 
at Chamberlain Park in Auckland.

I guess I agree that some tools are better than others, but a true craftsman 
understands the relationships and precisely what the tools do and how they 
do it. So it is with programming.

> One of the major and most missed concept of Software Engineering is
> language selection.  Maybe that's why the world is beginning to look
> at everything from an OOP perspective.  When that is the only thing
> you learn it tends to hold those blinders rather close to the eyes.

So, anyone who doesn't use COBOL is blind? :-)

I'm afraid that the reality I see when I look around does not match this 
model. I know (and have learned from) some very competent programmers who 
never wrote a line of COBOL in their lives. But I'd take bets that if you 
told them they MUST use COBOL for a specific job, they'd produce something 
excellent.

I knew a man once who walked onto a Burroughs site (having just come from 
ICL in Putney) picked up the Assembler manual for the B500, and then wrote 
an Operating System for it using that Assembler which he had never seen 
before in his life. And it never crashed. It was inspirational and was the 
day I learned that you can do anything in IT if you apply yourself and don't 
quit. I still have bad days when problems seem insoluble, but a few days 
later I'm laughing at them.

> and never has been. Furthermore, you can use the software for 
commercial
> development and distribute anything you build with these 
Editions, without
> paying any form of royalty or fee to Microsoft whatsoever or 
being
> constrined by the software being "nobbled".
> 
> As for "bells and whistles" you can only run SQL Server Express 
on ONE
> CPU, so if you want to start a server farm you probably will want 
to pay
> for the full version. However, most people, including businesses, 
are
> perfectly happy running it on a dedicated server. It networks over 
the LAN
> and the Internet extremely well and I regularly access remote 
and local
> instances of it (There... "instances", something that networked 
systems
> like to have...) I ran some performance comparisions yesterday 
between SQL
> Server and ORACLE. I'm not going into detail, but SQL server 
was around
> three times faster in most cases...It takes noticeably longer to 
connect,
> apart from anything else). I am NOT running either of them 
locally on a
> dedicated server; they run just as any other app on the machine. 
The same
> machine runs PostgreSQL, MySQL, MS Access 2003+, and SQL 
Lite when
> required to as well, although since I acquired SQL Server these 
have
> fallen into disuse and I retain them purely for testing the Toolset.)

#### ↳ Re: Interesting article

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-03-20T23:09:40+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9sr3b6Fer8U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<N5adnarR0OXzK_rSnZ2dnUVZ_rCdnZ2d@westnet.com.au>`

```
Paul Richards wrote:
> This may be of interest to some of the regular subscribers to this
> group:
>
> http://www.computerworld.com/s/article/9225079/Brain_drain_Where_Cobol_systems_go_from_here_

Thanks for posting this, Paul; I found it very interesting.

It is also a little frightening that people in such senior positions in 
large organisations simply don't get it.

It isn't a "brain drain" on the part of COBOL programmers that is causing 
the problem; it is failure by senior management to stay in touch and 
recognise that there is a sea change going on. It isn't like it happened 
overnight.

For over 20 years now it has been apparent that the paradigm has changed. 
For all of that time I have been telling COBOL guys to learn Java and expand 
their skill sets. (Maybe I should have been talking to managers, but I care 
more about programmers... :-))

Management should have seen what was coming and planned for it YEARS ago, 
long before Y2K.  (To be fair, in many smaller organisations they did; it is 
the big outfits who believed they were immune. Those same outfits are 
ponderous, resistant to change, and now wondering where to go next. So what 
do they do? Blame the programmers. They're all retiring... Gee, who could 
have predicted that?)

I can't find much sympathy for the leader of a major Bank who employs a 
couple of hundred COBOL guys and responds to the problem by deciding that 
they will keep on doing what they have always done. Or an IBM guy (with an 
obvious vested interest, whose attitude is "nothing to see here" and who 
patently doesn't get it either.) No imagination, no solution and no grasp of 
what the options are. Same old, same old... buy a new compiler, we'll look 
after you in COBOL...

Simply re-hosting COBOL on a network is NO solution and only a blind fool 
would think it is.

Converting existing procedural code to Java is also NO solution and any 
decent Java programmer will tell you WHY it isn't. It's like whisky and 
water; ruins two good things.

If there are priceless "untouchable" nuggets of business rules encapsulated 
in COBOL in the existing systems, the people to deal with that are the 
existing programmers who understand the Business. It isn't about outsourcing 
it to coders who have no knowledge, understanding, or perception of the 
business, just because they will work for $8 an hour...

Moving on from COBOL is NOT just a coding exercise in ANY language. If there 
is no strategic vision and the people tasked with making the decisions have 
no idea what is involved, how can it be anything other than a fiasco?

They need to look at the options and tools available, get skills and 
knowledge transfer going on while they still have people on site who 
understand the code, and they should be ensuring that the "rising 
generation" of programmers are involved with the legacy systems. It isn't 
hard for a modern programmer to learn the COBOL they need to know, and the 
best way to learn it is to work alongside an "old timer" who not only knows 
the language but knows the applications and the business. Certainly, the old 
timers may never understand OO or even want to, but the new breed do and 
they CAN re-wrap COBOL to migrate it, where it make sense to do so.

It isn't about a "war" between COBOL and Java (to be frank, it is no 
contest...), it is about programmers with different training and backgrounds 
working together to leverage old systems and salvage what is useful, in 
order to bring timely functionality to the business.

Reading that article just made me cringe at the really poor management that 
has led to the current situation in some places.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Interesting article

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-03-21T04:43:19+13:00

Fritz Wuehler wrote:
> Pete, would you care to recommend a good book or 2 on Java?

Well, the one I used initially (I bought other books on Java later) was: 
"SAMS Teach Yourself Java 2 in 24 hours" by Rogers Cadenhead.
It is published by www.samspublishing.com and the ISBN is: 0-672-31630-7

Cover price is $19.90 in the USA or 17.95 UK pounds. I paid $49.95 (NZ) for 
my copy, which is outrageous, but we get ripped off on books here... :-) 
Never mind, I consider it well spent.

I did the 24 lessons in the book and enjoyed every bit of it. It was printed 
in 1999 so there may be a later edition out now.

This book is the best technical book I have ever read on any subject. It is 
beautifully written and Cadenhead has a wicked sense of humour that had me 
chuckling while I learned.

I grappled with OO COBOL for several months trying to understand it and 
getting nowhere. Then I did this Java book and everything fell into place. I 
came back to OO COBOL and wondered why I ever thought it was difficult. :-)

Having both the Java and OO COBOL concepts as a base, it was very easy to 
learn C#, using free videos provided on the web.

The other Java books are all interesting but this one is a quantum leap 
above the rest.

HTH,

Pete.
```

I'm one of the "retiring" COBOL programmers.  Eight years ago I was told I would be losing my job in about 1 or 2 years because they were replacing the mainframe with a bunch of servers and going to use Microsoft Navision to run everything.  So I told them I'm leaving in September,  I'll be 66 in July, and now it's a race that I don't think they are going to win.  I've been here 38 years and have written every line of code being used on the mainframe.  I think there are some people around here who are worried a bunch.  Looks like I might be getting a raise for a change.<G>

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Interesting article

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-03-22T13:23:47+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9sv9olFufhU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<N5adnarR0OXzK_rSnZ2dnUVZ_rCdnZ2d@westnet.com.au> <7168845.479.1332266563383.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@ynjk1> <9st5j5Fn1cU1@mid.individual.net> <11407700.1260.1332333518627.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@vbxq27>`

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Wednesday, 21 March 2012 05:00:20 UTC, Pete Dashwood  wrote:
>> I quietly celebrated another birthday yesterday (and today - I was
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> So that makes you about 138 years old?

Some days I certainly feel it but I'm not quite 138 yet...

Harrumph! these young whippersnappers today... no respect... 
<snorkle...gurgle... descends into rhythmic snoring...>

One moderately quick story about my "two birthdays"...

Back in the 1960s NZ was part of a treaty betweeen Australia, NZ, and the 
USA (ANZUS) which was a mutual defence treaty; if any one of these parties 
was attacked, the others would help; I'm sure American citizens slept more 
soundly in their beds knowing that NZ had their backs and in the even of any 
trouble we could send a leaky boat full of hairy arsed Rugby forwards to 
defend them...

(An ant and an elephant are walking across a swing bridge. The ant says: 
"Man! Are we swinging this bridge...")

Anyway, there was some unpleasantness going on in Asia at the time and the 
US Government needed some moral support so we started training young men to 
shoot at people in black pyjamas.  (This seems perfectly fair to me, on the 
grounds of poor fashion taste, apart from any political motivation.)

Kiwis generally are not too keen to kill people and we don't have a lot of 
guns in our society. (It is illegal to own a handgun here; sporting rifles 
and shotguns only, although I believe a gun club can get permission for 
members to have hand guns, provided certain very stringent requirements are 
met. Cops don't carry guns and have only recently been issued tasers. Due to 
an alarming increase in armed offending cops now have access to guns but 
don't carry sidearms. At the time I'm writing about (the late 1960s) 
American television had not worked its magic on the fabric of our society 
and cops didn't actually need guns...)

So people weren't exactly flocking to get sent off to a hot steamy jungle 
where your life was likely to be in danger.

Conscription had to be in place but it was only tolerated because we had 
commitments. The system worked as follows:

1. 19 year old males registered for military service.
2.  When they turned 20, their birthday was put into a barrel
3. There was a Lotto style draw of a set number of brthdays. If yours was 
drawn you would become a soldier. (At least for a while; your employer was 
required by Law to keep your job for you. Mine didn't and I could have sued, 
but it was easier to just get anoither job...)

As I didn't want to go to Viet Nam (on the grounds of having nothing against 
the Vietnamese, allied with a genetic predisposition to not looking for 
trouble) I didn't think it was fair I should have two chances in the raffle, 
when everybody else only had one.

I spoke out about this and the Registration Staff Sergeant (who I later 
found to be a very fair-minded man...) said: "OK, pick one."

I picked the 20th March (on the simple grounds that it was first in the 
series).

The 21st of March was not drawn; the 20th of March was.

The Company I was working for had it deferred on the grounds that I was "key 
personnel" but it could not be deferred indefinitely and I eventually had to 
do it.

Once I got into it, I actually quite enjoyed it even though I never got to 
shoot an any people in black pyjamas.

NZ, being a meritocracy, they tested all 700 odd of us for personality and 
IQ (in some cases they found little of either) and sent the top 5% for 
Officer training.  I was in the Signal Corp (they reckoned a computer 
programmer should be able to lay wire on a battlefield under intense fire) 
and, as an officer, I was allowed to use a sten gun. (A very primitive, 
inaccurate, weapon by today's standards; it cost two shillings and sixpence 
to make, was an unrifled tube with a magazine and trigger attached and 
worked on the optimistic principle that accuracy was unimportant as long as 
you fired lots of rounds... BUT, it was an automatic weapon and that was SO 
COOL!)

They told me that if I was prepared to join up permanently, they would 
ratify my acting commission and I could be a career soldier. I told them I 
wasn't into acting, couldn't swear an Oath of Allegiance to Queen Elizabeth 
the Second and her heirs and successors (sorry Ma'am, it isn't personal...), 
and received all the aggravation I needed in my life from my wife, who was 
very upset about me being in the Army, and probably wouldn't go for it as a 
career choice.

I still have a faded photograph of myself in uniform, along with the boys of 
7 platoon... Hey! wasn't that Custer's outfit?

Ah, happy times... :-)

Pete.
```

```
On Thu, 22 Mar 2012 13:23:47 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>Alistair Maclean wrote:
>> On Wednesday, 21 March 2012 05:00:20 UTC, Pete Dashwood  wrote:
…[95 more quoted lines elided]…
>Pete.

Thanks for sharing that story Pete.  As one who was conscripted and
sent to shoot at people in black pajamas, I can relate to your tale. I
had heard there were Aussies and Kiwis in 'Nam but never got to work
along side of them.  I did work with some South Koreans a little.
Nasty, very serious bunch.  Woke up every day before anyone else and
did martial arts training even in the middle of the jungle.

We also had "tests" in the Army before being slotted into a career.
First they offered me a chance to go to helicopter pilot school after
basic training.  It would have been cool to be a Warrant Officer (a
position between the highest NCO and the lowest CO..kind of like being
in limbo) and getting paid more.  And how cool would it have been to
learn to fly helicopters?  But then I saw the statistics on the life
expectancy of a helo pilot in 'Nam.  Something under 10 seconds as I
recall.  Plus I would have had to commit to at least 1 more year in
the service.  Pass.  Then they offered me NCO school.  No longer
commitment, would have become a sergeant out of the school but I had
to go right after advanced infantry training instead of going home on
leave for 30 days.  As I was missing my wife and child, I passed on
that also.  So I just went and did my time and stayed alive and then
rose hell about the war when I got out of the Army.

Regards,
```

```
SkippyPB wrote:
> On Thu, 22 Mar 2012 13:23:47 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
<snipped>
> Thanks for sharing that story Pete.  As one who was conscripted and
> sent to shoot at people in black pajamas, I can relate to your tale. I
> had heard there were Aussies and Kiwis in 'Nam but never got to work
> along side of them.

As you know Steve, the whole "war" was a fiasco. Australia gave conscripts 6 
weeks basic training then put them in the jungle against some of the finest 
jungle fighters in the world. Thousands of young Australians were killed or 
wounded. Many fled the Country and I shared a flat in Auckland with a 
Queenslander who was one of the gentlest people I have ever met and who 
would not have lasted 5 minutes in the jungle.

New Zealand sent only full-time professional soldiers who were volunteers. 
Our main contribution was an artillery battery equipped with 105 mm pack 
howitzers. The strength was a little over a hundred men and they spent most 
of their time shelling the Americans. They reckoned they dropped fire on the 
co-ordinates they were given; the people on the receiving end had a 
different view and the truth will never be known. Friendly fire is actually 
decidedly unfriendly and the worst part is that it is coming from people who 
are supposed to be Allies. Nobody wins in War.

Although we were trained for Viet Nam and expected to go, by 1969 it was 
becoming less likely that we would actually go.

I didn't let on to the wife, but I was little disappointed. By then we were 
all gung ho to get into the adventure and when you're in your early twenties 
you believe you are indestructible... Sitting here now with the benefit of 
hindsight I can see I had a lucky escape and am just saddened by the toll on 
all sides that such a stupid and pointless escapade took on all the 
participants. I have nothing but respect for the people who went and I think 
the treatment many of them received on returning home was shabby and 
despicable.

>  I did work with some South Koreans a little.
> Nasty, very serious bunch.  Woke up every day before anyone else and
> did martial arts training even in the middle of the jungle.

Ah, those inscrutable Orientals... :-)

>
> We also had "tests" in the Army before being slotted into a career.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> the service.  Pass.

I don't blame you. Maybe OK if you have no family and responsibility only to 
yourself, but not otherwise...

>Then they offered me NCO school.  No longer
> commitment, would have become a sergeant out of the school but I had
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> rose hell about the war when I got out of the Army.

Thanks for that Steve. Very interesting.

On the positive side, you can say you were a part of twentieth century 
history and you did your duty honourably. There were many who didn't and 
they probably justify it with the stupidity of the war. But they still know 
deep down inside that they didn't meet the challenge, and they have to live 
with that.

I remember playing music with some young Americans who were on R & R in 
Auckland. One of them was born on the same day of the same year as I was. It 
was just circumstance that he was in Nam and I wasn't. We played some Blues 
and had a few beers and reflected on the peculiarities of time and space.

He was killed in action 3 months later.

It took me quite a while to get over it, even though I didn't know him well. 
It's like I said before, nobody wins...

Pete.
```

Too right. I've given quite a bit of money to veterans organizations. I feel
it's the least I can do. But count yourself lucky. Nobody came back
unchanged and most didn't come back normal. I read more American Vietnam vets
committed suicide than were killed in the war. If you get to Washington
D.C. I bet anybody he can't go see the Wall and not cry, I don't care how
big and tough he is. If he has a heart, when he sees all that misery, all
those thousands and thousands of names of people's sons and brothers and
fathers who never came home, and how people are still laying wreaths and
flowers and bawling, he won't be able not to join in. The destruction of war
is incalculable, the effects go on for generations. The worst part is mostly
it's about politics and money and they are willing to kill and maim for
those goals. Disgusting...

I've a few friends that went through it and some of them are successful some
of them not but they're all wounded inside and it never goes away. You can
tell by the look in their eyes once you've seen one of the poor fellows.

> 
> >  I did work with some South Koreans a little.
> > Nasty, very serious bunch.  Woke up every day before anyone else and
> > did martial arts training even in the middle of the jungle.

I heard from an old man who was in Korea they were the toughest of the
lot. And it was real hard because it was so damn cold. I don't remember the
exact story but it went something like "the Japanese will chase you for a
mile, the Thai for 5 miles, but the Koreans don't give up until they catch
you and kill you or they die on the hoof running".

> > First they offered me a chance to go to helicopter pilot school after
> > basic training.  It would have been cool to be a Warrant Officer (a
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> > the service.  Pass.

I heard the average tour of a chopper pilot over there was 3 weeks from a
guy who was an translator/interrogater. He wrote a 250 page book and gave it
to me and then when I gave it back after reading it he ran it through his
shredder in front of me. Damn good book, horrible, but good. He had to get
it out of his system. I wish I hadn't read it but for his sake I'm glad I
did. I'm still wondering how I'll get it out of my system. Guess I should go
buy a shredder of my own.

> It's like I said before, nobody wins...

Nope

On Thu, 22 Mar 2012 20:20:22 +0100 (CET), Nomen Nescio
<nobody@dizum.com> wrote:

>"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>

To tell the truth, when I was drafted I barely knew about the war in
Vietnam other than to realize when I did get my draft notice where I'd
be going.  I had other things going on in my life and just didn't pay
that much attention to the war.  Had I been more aware I may have left
for Canada.  I don't know.  I never really thought about it.  


>Too right. I've given quite a bit of money to veterans organizations. I feel
>it's the least I can do. But count yourself lucky. Nobody came back
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Here's a story I heard about the South Koreans in Vietnam.  I'm pretty
sure it is true.  We knew them as ROKs (Republic of Korea).  One of
their areas of responsibility was a resort town on the South China sea
coast named Vung Tau.  They did all of the security for that city.  It
was an in-country R&R destination.  We were allowed 3 days in-country
R&R during any 12 month stay.  I was unable to take advantage of it
myself but did do a 7 day R&R to Hawaii.  Anyway, the story goes that
an NVA (North Vietnamese Army) unit snuck into the area and dropped
some rockets in on Vung Tau.  I don't know if there were any
casualties or not but it incensed the ROKs.  They set out to find the
NVA unit and a platoon of ROKs chased a company sized unit of NVA
across Vietnam and almost into Cambodia before finally catching up to
and killing every last one of them.  Vung Tau was never bombed,
attacked or rocketed again.


>> > First they offered me a chance to go to helicopter pilot school after
>> > basic training.  It would have been cool to be a Warrant Officer (a
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Due to how I handled myself and kept my mental sanity while I was in
country, I never suffered any PTSD afterwards.  Being part of the
Vietnam Veterans Against the War and visiting vets in the vet
hospitals also helped.  They could talk to us because we could relate
and I believe it helped both ways.  

There were several movies made about the Vietnam War and I had no
trouble watching them.  Most were unreal especially Green Berets.
However, Platoon came the closest to depicting what it was like, at
least from my point of view, in Vietnam and to this day I cannot watch
that movie again.

>> It's like I said before, nobody wins...
>
>Nope

Very true especially contrived wars like Vietnam and Iraq and
Afghanistan.

Regards,
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Interesting article

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2012-03-23T15:58:50-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<11502363.1693.1332543530478.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@ynjl17>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<bb4pm71q5ql2p813a43eh6qte0p0cm5qtc@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<9t107aFnr5U1@mid.individual.net> <cc21224c606af8c39f39029c5099df94@dizum.com> <bb4pm71q5ql2p813a43eh6qte0p0cm5qtc@4ax.com>`

```
On Friday, March 23, 2012 11:23:42 AM UTC-4, SkippyPB wrote:
[snip]

> Here's a story I heard about the South Koreans in Vietnam.  I'm pretty
> sure it is true.  We knew them as ROKs (Republic of Korea).  One of
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> attacked or rocketed again.

While I have no desire to refute the story, it was
not likely Vung Tau. While I spent only one night
there and saw only US troops, I understood that
it was a base for the Aussies.

=====
< http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vung_Tau >

During the Vietnam War, Vũng Tàu was home to the
Australian Army and American support units, and
was a popular spot for in-country R&R for U.S.
combat troops. 
=====

Confirms what I understood.

< http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Republic_of_Korea_Marine_Corps >
states: The Republic of Korea Army units' Tactical
Area of Responsibility were the southern half of
the I Corps. The ROK Marines were deployed with
the I Corps alongside US Marines.

< http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/I_Corps_(South_Vietnam) >
states: The I Corps Tactical Zone was a corps of the Army
of the Republic of Vietnam (ARVN), the army of the nation
state of South Vietnam that existed from 1955 to 1975.
It was one of four corps which the ARVN oversaw. This
was the northernmost region of South Vietnam, bordering
North Vietnam.

Vung Tau was south of Saigon; I believe in III Corp.

Don't hate me but for my 18-months in-country,
I was stationed at Long Binh. See
< http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Long_Binh >.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Interesting article

_(thread depth: 10)_

- **From:** SkippyPB <swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com>
- **Date:** 2012-03-24T12:21:22-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<njrrm796a7l8v3v5sbaheavsn07jrnt30q@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<9t107aFnr5U1@mid.individual.net> <cc21224c606af8c39f39029c5099df94@dizum.com> <bb4pm71q5ql2p813a43eh6qte0p0cm5qtc@4ax.com> <11502363.1693.1332543530478.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@ynjl17>`

```
On Fri, 23 Mar 2012 15:58:50 -0700 (PDT), Rick Smith
<rs847925@gmail.com> wrote:

>On Friday, March 23, 2012 11:23:42 AM UTC-4, SkippyPB wrote:
>[snip]
…[50 more quoted lines elided]…
>< http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Long_Binh >.

Could very well be true.  It was just a story one of the two tour
sergeants related to us.  Areas of operation changed some during the
years of the conflict and it could have changed for the ROKs.    Vung
Tau was indeed in III Corps.  I plied my trade in II Corps. 

However, there maybe some validity to the story I told.  In May 1964,
Major General Norman B. Edwards, Chief, US Joint Military Advisory
Group, Korea, began preliminary planning to send a Korean Mobile Army
Surgical Hospital to Vietnam. The Korean Ministry of Defense (MOD) and
the United Nations Command (UNC) agreed. A survey team of six Korean
and five US officers left the ROK in August 1964 for Vietnam. In
September an agreement was reached for the Koreans to send and operate
a Mobile Army Surgical Hospital (MASH) unit in the RVN. The deployment
of the ROK Army (ROKA) 1st MASH began in September 1964, first
activated at Vung Tau.

The ROK Military Assistance Group, Vietnam (ROK-MAG-V), and what came
to be known as the ï¿½Peace Dove Unitï¿½ were set up between February and
June 1965. It would consist of 2,416 men and women from a Korean
construction support group ("Pigeon Unit"), a Korean Marine Corps
engineer company, Korean Navy Tank Landing Ships (LSTs) and Landing
Ships Mechanized (LSMs), and a Korean Army security company. This
would grow in short order to an Army engineer battalion; an Army
transport company; a Marine engineer company; one LST with crew; a
security battalion; a service unit; a liaison group, and a mobile
hospital (already in Vietnam). An aerial support group was set up
later.

ROK-MAG(V) was headquartered at Bien Hoa, near Saigon. The "Peace
Doves" built many school buildings, freeways, roads, and bridges for
the Vietnamese in the RVN.

The ROK then sent three combat divisions to the RVN over a 12 month
period starting in September 1965. 

The ROK sent the Capital "Tiger Division" to Qui Nhon, and the 2nd
Marine "Blue Dragon" Brigade to Cam Ranh Bay and then to Tuy Hoa to
get things started in 1965. Then, starting in April 1966, they sent in
the 9th ROK "White Horse" Division. Once done, the Marine Brigade
could move into the northernmost I Corps to join with the US III
Marine Amphibious Force (III MAF). In turn, the Koreans created a
Korean Army Corps that extended from Binh Dinh Province south to Ninh
Thuan Province along the RVN's coastline.

The Blue Dragon Marine Brigade was positioned at Cam Ran Bay, in
southeastern Khan Hoa Province, then moved to Tuy Hoa, then moved to I
Corps.  

Cam Ran Bay was in III Corps along with Vung Tau.

A very good website that details ROK involvement in Vietnam can be
found at:

http://www.talkingproud.us/Military/ROKVIetnam/ROKVIetnamIntro.html

Regards,
```

```
On Saturday, March 24, 2012 12:21:22 PM UTC-4, SkippyPB wrote:
> On Fri, 23 Mar 2012 15:58:50 -0700 (PDT), Rick Smith
> <rs847...@gmail.com> wrote:
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> >> attacked or rocketed again.
[snip]
> A very good website that details ROK involvement in Vietnam can be
> found at:
> 
> http://www.talkingproud.us/Military/ROKVIetnam/ROKVIetnamIntro.html

I had just read that page before seeing your post.

Out of curiosity I checked out Australia's involvement
as well. (About six hours of reading.)

I then found references to rocket attacks on Vung Tau
on Tet '68 and in '69. I think I'll just treat the
story as apocryphal and let it go at that.

The only interesting 'war' story involving me is that
I went over as a truck driver. I went on some ride-alongs
and after three weeks I was assigned a truck. The next
morning several of us joined a convoy. Just north of
Long Binh is a long grade. My truck didn't have enough
power to hold speed on the climb, so I was ordered to
return. When I started down the hill, I realized that
the brakes weren't working very well, either. So, for
a few seconds, I frantically pumped the braked pedal
and gained enough control to make it safely down the
hill. My cargo was 5000 gallons of diesel fuel and for
those few seconds 'fiery crash' was on my mind!

Later that day, I was called in to see the First Sergeant.
It seems the Company Clerk was 'helmet-on-boots short'
and I could type, so I became the Company Clerk and
spent the next seventeen months 'driving' a typewriter.

Never fired a shot and seldom carried a rifle!
One consequence is that, even though I am a Vietnam
Veteran, I cannot relate to what others went through.
```

```
On Sat, 24 Mar 2012 21:44:25 -0700 (PDT), Rick Smith
<rs847925@gmail.com> wrote:

>On Saturday, March 24, 2012 12:21:22 PM UTC-4, SkippyPB wrote:
>> On Fri, 23 Mar 2012 15:58:50 -0700 (PDT), Rick Smith
…[40 more quoted lines elided]…
>Veteran, I cannot relate to what others went through.

Long Binh is where I entered country in '68.  Thought they'd hand me
an M-16 coming off of the plane and send me on my way.  Little did I
know!

One of my duties once I was elevated to the fire command center was to
drive the command track...an APC with a covered back high enough for
Wilt Chamberlin to stand up in!  We were driving around some hills on
our way to a firebase somewhere.  The ground was very soft and the
trail was made for water buffalo pulling carts not APCs.  The normal
APCs were not having too much trouble but my big command track wanted
to tumble down the mountain on the right side.  Some how I kept the
damn thing upright and we made to our destination.  I think they had
to pry my hands off of the control sticks!

As for being a company clerk, I too took typing in high school and it
got me out of going to bivouac in the middle of winter in Kentucky
during basic training.  It also got me a cushy job as company clerk
when I did my last 6 months at Fort Carson.  Almost stayed in the Army
but they wouldn't guarantee me that position if I signed up for
another 4 years.

Regards,
```

```
On Sunday, March 25, 2012 1:14:11 PM UTC-4, SkippyPB wrote:
[snip]
> Long Binh is where I entered country in '68.  Thought they'd hand me
> an M-16 coming off of the plane and send me on my way.  Little did I
> know!

My time was Oct69 thru May71, including the 30-day leave
for the 6-month extension.

Long Binh was THE place for replacements. When my older
brother (Army Capt, pilot, fixed-wing) arrived in Jan71,
I met him at the replacement company. He was assigned to
a company at Bien Hoa, 5-miles away. He was later
transferred to Long Binh, less than two miles from me.
We got together a few times, but we were both busy,
there was, after all, a war going on! I did spend one
day with him where we flew up the coast stopping at a
few airfields to deliver some communications equipment.

Yesterday I told a younger brother about this thread and
he said that he had tried to get a transfer to Vietnam
while I was there, but was denied. It seems the rule
prohibiting family members from serving in the same
combat area applied without exception to enlisted, but
only conditionally to officers.

Now, a suitable COBOL reference:
< http://www.foxprohistory.org/interview_wayne_ratliff.htm >
=====
Susan Lammers: Did you go to Vietnam? 

Wayne Ratliff: No. As a result of the Martin Marietta job,
I had a civilian acquired skill, so I programmed. For two
years I worked on a logistics war game called LOGEX,
programming in COBOL. Most of my work was related to
ordering equipment and supplies. It was like doing an
inventory for an extremely large company, except there
are unusual military policies you have to deal
within--like nuclear weapons requiring presidential
authorization.
=====
```

```
On Monday, March 26, 2012 9:21:32 PM UTC-4, James Gavan wrote:
> On 3/26/2012 7:55 AM, Rick Smith wrote:
> (snip>
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> was repeated in the movie "Saving Private Ryan".

Actually, the deaths of those siblings was the precedent
for the Sole Survivor Policy. The policy concerning
assignment to Vietnam was unprecedented and may have
been an attempt to extend the policy to provide
additional safeguards. In any case, the current policy,
as contained in Army Regulation 614-30, does not
include such a provision for Hostile Fire/Imminent
Danger Areas.

For my part, I was only vaguely aware of the policy and
asked my older brother, when he arrived, why he didn't
request deferment. He said it would hurt his career plans.

But, upon a cursory review of the Army's selection policies,
as described in a court case from that era, it seems unlikely
that my younger brother would ever have been assigned to
Vietnam. His Military Occupational Specialty was not, and
ultimately was never, used in Vietnam. I'll discuss this
with him Sunday.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Interesting article

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>

```
On Thursday, March 22, 2012 3:20:22 PM UTC-4, Nomen Nescio wrote:
[snip]
> I've a few friends that went through it and some of them are successful some
> of them not but they're all wounded inside and it never goes away. You can
> tell by the look in their eyes once you've seen one of the poor fellows.

In 2007, I had occasion to be in the Mental Heath Clinic
at the Gainesville (FL) VA Medical Center and met a fellow
Vet who, forty years later, was still hearing, as he put it,
the 'distinctive sounds' of AK-47 gun fire. Having never
heard an AK-47 and never having been in the situation of
there being others shooting at me, I couldn't relate; but
did see that he was troubled.

My sole experience with the sound of war occurred around
Tet '70 when our Battalion area was hit by, what was
identified as, six RPGs. It happened during daylight hours,
didn't cause any serious damage, and only one minor injury;
but did get my blood pumping. I live near an Interstate
highway and occasionally hear the sound of truck tires
exploding. The first time I heard one of these explosions
I was more than curious what it was, but did not 'flash
black' to 1970.
```

>SkippyPB wrote:
>> On Thu, 22 Mar 2012 13:23:47 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>

In the US Army (and Marines as well) you received 8 weeks basic
training.  Then, if you were a designated as an infantry soldier
(which most were) you got another 8 weeks advanced training.  After
that, if you weren't going on to NCO school or Rangers or anything
like that, you went home for a leave and then off to your foreign
assignment.   85% of my class in Advanced Infantry went to Vietnam,
the others to South Korea and Germany.

Once I got with my unit in Vietnam, I got another week of training -
more of a refresher course if you will and as a way of acclimating you
to the terrain.  

Still, with all of the training, it doesn't prepare you for that first
ambush you are caught in or the first fire mission (I was in mortars)
you execute or the first straight on assault of your security line by
the enemy.  You either shit your pants and then do your job or do your
job and puke later.  If the former, you'll never make it.  If the
latter, you've got a good chance.  And the more you are involved the
easier it becomes.  Some even shred their fear altogether and yearn
for it.  It is an adrenaline rush.  Personally, I was able to temper
my fear and do my job and worry about being scared when all was said
and done.


>New Zealand sent only full-time professional soldiers who were volunteers. 
>Our main contribution was an artillery battery equipped with 105 mm pack 
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I experienced friendly fire while there.  Even though I was in a
self-propelled 4.2 Mortar platoon we were too often placed with the
RECON platoon instead of being a mile or so away from them so that we
could fire mortars for them if needed.  The main reason, or so we were
told, that we traveled and stayed with RECON and other Infantry
companies was because our APCs (Armored Personnel Carriers) had 50
caliber machine guns on them.  We also carried M-16 rifles and a few
of us had either 38 or 45 caliber handguns.

On one such mission into the jungle with a RECON and Infantry company
we started taking in fire from across a rather large pond.  A 4.2
mortar has to travel 900 meters before it is armed and the kind folks
shooting at us were too close.  We blanketed the area with machine gun
fire but the rockets kept coming in.  So our platoon leader called in
an artillery strike.  I believe it was 105 artillery that was charged
with dropping rounds in.  The first landed right in the middle of our
camp.  Only about 1000 or so meters off!  A couple of guys in the
infantry unit were wonder but luckily no one was killed.  After
frantic, vulgarity ridden radio calls between the Fire Ops Chief at
the artillery unit and our platoon leader, the next rounds were on
target.  A subsequent sweep through the target area yielded nothing so
they probably shot off all of their rockets and got out of there
before the artillery hit the mark.

>Although we were trained for Viet Nam and expected to go, by 1969 it was 
>becoming less likely that we would actually go.
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>

For anyone who might be interested, here's some websites with pictures
from the area I was in while in Vietnam.  I was in the 4th Infantry
division and worked with these groups at various times during my 12
months there.

http://www.printroom.com/ViewGallery.asp?shopperid=1&userid=caviews&gallery_id=1276294

http://jh.photoshelter.com/gallery/4th-Infantry-Division-Vietnam-1967-1968/G0000CkqjaZHW8HM/

Here's a link to the history of my Battalion.  

http://www.globalsecurity.org/military/agency/army/2-8in.htm



>>  I did work with some South Koreans a little.
>> Nasty, very serious bunch.  Woke up every day before anyone else and
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Had I been more astute, more politically aware back then I may not
have done my duty.  It took going there, being there, to wake up and
then I still didn't get it until I met a Quaker guy from the American
Friends Service Committee who got me to research the history and
reason for Vietnam.  Then the light went on.


>I remember playing music with some young Americans who were on R & R in 
>Auckland. One of them was born on the same day of the same year as I was. It 
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I could have gone to Sydney on R&R and if I had we might have crossed
paths!  I played music before and during high school but not much
afterwords but I always gravitated toward the live music pubs.

I was lucky in that no one in my platoon was killed during the year I
was in 'Nam.  We had a couple of guys wounded bad enough to be sent
home, but that was it.  I spent some time in a M.A.S.H. unit due to
being burned.  I learned that 2 guys were killed in an ambush a month
or so after I left.


>It took me quite a while to get over it, even though I didn't know him well. 
>It's like I said before, nobody wins...
>
>Pete.

In war it is not who wins but who is left.

Regards,
```

```
SkippyPB wrote:
> On Fri, 23 Mar 2012 04:53:12 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[178 more quoted lines elided]…
> Regards,

Many thanks for posting these thoughts and experiences, Steve.

All of us are the sum total of our experiences and sometimes, although we 
don't see it at the time, there is value even in very hard times.

I sincerely hope the shell dropped on your guys was NOT from my countrymen, 
but in case it was, let me say here and now on behalf of all of us, 
"Sorry..." :-)

I remember my father telling me a "war story" that happened in the Second 
World War. In those days they used to fly Sunderland Flying boats between 
Australia and New Zealand and although the service was curtailed during the 
hostilities, it operated regularly from 1940 until 1947 and was 
re-instituted later.

There was a considerable number of American warships berthed in Wellington 
and Auckland and apparently, on one occasion in Wellington, one of the 
Sunderlands drew fire as it came in to land. It was a brief burst fired from 
one of the ships docked at the wharves and nobody seemed to know exactly 
which ship...The aircraft was slightly damaged and the NZ Government was 
furious and demanded an investigation and a full report on how this could 
happen. The American Naval Commander carried out his own investigation and 
duly reported:

"If it was one of theirs, we did it; if it was one of ours, we know nothing 
about it."

Pete.
```

## Thread: The Oneness of God is the message of Jesus and all the Prophets, peace be upon them !!!!!!!!!!!!!! — 1 msgs

### The Oneness of God is the message of Jesus and all the Prophets, peace be upon them !!!!!!!!!!!!!!

- **From:** BV BV <bv8bv8bv8@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2012-03-24T11:02:25-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8ce70e81-49eb-4cea-a16c-59c840e5037f@w5g2000vbv.googlegroups.com>`

```
The Oneness of God is the message of Jesus and all the Prophets, peace
be upon them


The message that was brought by the Messiah (peace be upon him) was
the call to worship God, the One, the Lord of the Messiah and the Lord
of the worlds:

"Now this is eternal life: that they may know you, the only true God,
and Jesus Christ, whom you have sent"
John 17:3 - NIV

"A certain ruler asked him: 'Good teacher, what must I do to inherit
eternal life?'
'Why do you call me good?' Jesus answered. 'No one is good - except
God alone.'"
Luke 18:18-19 - NIV


"The devil led him up to a high place and showed him in an instant all
the kingdoms of the world.
And he said to him, 'I will give you all their authority and
splendour, for it has been given to me, and I can give it to anyone I
want to.
So if you worship me, it will all be yours.'
Jesus answered, 'It is written: "Worship the Lord your God and serve
him only."'"
Luke 4:5-8 - NIV


Believing in and worshipping Allaah alone, besides Whom there is no
other god, is the greatest teaching brought by the Messiah, and it is
the greatest teaching brought by all the Prophets.

"One of the teachers of the law came and noticed them debating.
Noticing that Jesus had given them a good answer, he asked him, 'Of
all the commandments, which is the most important?'
'The most important one,' answered Jesus, 'is this: "Hear, O Israel,
the Lord our God, the Lord is one.
Love the Lord your God with all your heart and with all your soul and
with all your mind and with all your strength."

The second is this: "Love your neighbour as yourself." There is no
commandment greater than these.'
'Well said, teacher,' the man replied. 'You are right in saying that
God is one and there is no other but him.
To love him with all your heart, with you all your understanding and
with all your strength, and to love your neighbour as yourself is more
important that all burnt offerings and sacrifices.'
When Jesus saw that he has answered wisely, he said to him, 'You are
not far from the kingdom of God.' And from then on no one dared ask
him any more questions."
Mark 12:28-34 - NIV


But do not think that this advice was given to Israel or to his own
people only. Rather this is the basis of the teachings of all the
Prophets. The same advice appears in the Gospel of Matthew, in similar
wording, after which he says:
"All the Law and the Prophets hang on these two commandments."
Matthew 22:39 - NIV


This belief in the oneness of God is indeed the message of all of the
Prophets.

Allaah says (interpretation of the meaning):
"And We did not send any Messenger before you (O Muhammad) but We
revealed to him (saying): Laa ilaaha illa Ana [none has the right to
be worshipped but I (Allaah)], so worship Me (Alone and none else)"

This is the basic message to which the Messiah called people and
warned them against differing from that.

"Surely, they have disbelieved who say: 'Allaah is the Messiah ['Eesa
(Jesus)], son of Maryam (Mary).' But the Messiah ['Eesa (Jesus)] said:

whosoever sets up partners (in worship) with Allaah, then Allaah has
forbidden Paradise to him, and the Fire will be his abode. And for the
Zaalimoon (polytheists and wrongdoers) there are no helpers"
[al-Maa'idah 5:72]


This is the basic principle on which we should all agree.

Allaah says (interpretation of the meaning):
"Say (O Muhammad): 'O people of the Scripture (Jews and Christians):
Come to a word that is just between us and you, that we worship none

they turn away, say: 'Bear witness that we are Muslims'"
[Aal 'Imraan 3:64]


It is alien to true Christianity, this futile attempt to reconcile the
belief in the Oneness of God, which is the message brought by the
Prophets, and which is clearly stated in their Bible, and affirmed in
the Torah in particular, with their belief in the trinity.

It says in the American Encyclopedia:
The belief in the Oneness of God - as a theological movement - began
at a very early stage in history, and in fact it preceded the belief
in trinity by many decades. Christianity developed from Judaism, and
Judaism firmly believes that there is one God.
The path that led from Jerusalem (the home of the first disciples of
Christ) to Nicea (where it was decided in 325 CE that Christ was equal
to God in essence and eternal nature) can hardly be described as a
straight path.
The doctrine of trinity which was affirmed in the fourth century CE
bears no resemblance to the original teachings of Christ concerning
the nature of God. Au contraire, it is the opposite, a deviation from
that teaching. Hence it developed in opposition to the belief in One
God...
(27/294).

believe in the Oneness of God in the sameAmerican Encyclopedia,
27/300-301
It is difficult to comprehend, so no wonder you were never able to
comprehend it. But what is strange is that you believe in something

we will understand it more clearly in the future, when the veil is

<a id="t-3181f8fe8d"></a>
## Thread: End of the OS ? The Monster Mash — 3 this month / 8 total

### End of the OS ? The Monster Mash

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-03-28T11:34:43+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9tetk5Fdh9U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
It just occurred to me that some people will have no idea what a Mashup is.

Here is a short, but very accurate video.

http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=U9sENSA_sjI

(The presenter says that around 2.5 Mashups a day were being produced at the 
time the video was made and he predicted 10 a day by 2010. The most credible 
figures I can find, suggest that it is currently around 6 a day, but it is 
increasing...)


The fact is that not only technology, but also the way in which people use 
technology is changing rapidly.

45 years ago I used to wish that people could get access to computers 
without having to go through the barrier of analysts and programmers. If 
only everyone could just get the support and computing power they needed, 
because the interface was so simple, imagine the apps they could produce...

I spent hours writing natural language interfaces for DOS based PCs. (One of 
them was called "IGOR" and it was sold in Sydney Australia, on DOS based 
systems. IGOR helped non-technical people run their applications and 
provided help and guidance through conversation. It was very well received 
ad over 400 machines with it on were sold in a single weekend when it first 
became available. When Windows arrived the point and click interface 
rendered IGOR largely redundant although I continued to receive mail from 
people who enjoyed IGOR for a few years afterwards.)

It was this experience which first stimulated my interest in getting 
computers to write their own code and to generate from templates. (I first 
did this on a mainframe site in Spain, and it enabled people to get their 
own reports without needing to request them from Programming.)

Films like "2001 - a Space Odyssey" with the fully conversational HAL (IBM - 
1) 9000 just made me drool for such a system. (OK, I know HAL was "insane", 
driven to madness by the enormity of the Mission, but us programmers know 
that a computer could never REALLY become the effect of its own data, right? 
:-))

Now, with Mashups, it looks as if we are getting close to a truly 
non-specialist interface for machines and the fact that High School students 
are pursuing it enthusiastically and producing some very impressive results 
gives me a warm feeling.

I'm glad I lived long enough to see this.

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: End of the OS ? The Monster Mash

- **From:** Alistair Maclean <alistair.j.l.maclean@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2012-03-28T07:47:02-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<17919517.1059.1332946022222.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@vbex14>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<9tetk5Fdh9U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<9tetk5Fdh9U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Tuesday, 27 March 2012 23:34:43 UTC+1, Pete Dashwood  wrote:
> It just occurred to me that some people will have no idea what a Mashup is.
> 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

<BIG SNIP>

> Now, with Mashups, it looks as if we are getting close to a truly 
> non-specialist interface for machines and the fact that High School students 
> are pursuing it enthusiastically and producing some very impressive results 
> gives me a warm feeling.

One problem woth mashups sourcing content from other APIs and Mashups is that when your source changes or is demised then your content disappears. Another little problem is that I would not trust a Mashup which was developed in someone's back bedroom by a <worst case scenario being painted here> person who did not understand any of the basic concepts about programming, design, ease-of-use, etc., which we already take for granted. Imagine a script kiddy writing MS Windows; would it work? Would you trust your livelihood to a badly designed/written and inadequately tested Mashup? I certainly wouldn't trust anyone who presented a video in which it was patently obvious that he did not know how to spell GOOGLE.
```

#### ↳ Re: End of the OS ? The Monster Mash

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2012-03-28T11:43:47-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<02b9f706-f238-4842-bbbc-106fd842419e@ur9g2000pbc.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<9tetk5Fdh9U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Mar 28, 11:34 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> It just occurred to me that some people will have no idea what a Mashup is.
>
> Here is a short, but very accurate video.

I am not sure why you think it was 'accurate'. It was just hype from a
Zdnet editor trying to generate interest in his camp and his blogs.


> Now, with Mashups, it looks as if we are getting close to a truly
> non-specialist interface for machines

I am not sure why you think that this is 'truly non-specialist'.
Mashups still require programming, web pages and Javascript, possibly
server based stuff too. It is done using APIs, certainly they are web-
based APIs, but the code that uses those APIs doesn't write itself.

What may finally happen with these APIs that Mashups use is that we
will get what Netscape was working towards in the early 90s and that
is a browser based system where the internet is the computer. That
frightened Microsoft, which had ignored the internet*, into ripping
off Spyglass** to get IE written and then giving it away for free and
paying OEMs to remove Netscape. Netscape was driven out of business
and so the desktop computer survived and held back the industry for
another 20 years.

Now, eventually, we are moving towards Netscape's vision with cloud
computing. The APIs which mashups use are mechanisms to get data and
process this. It is no different in concept from a program that uses,
say, a database API and a charting library API (neither of which are
part of the OS) to produce a bar chart. The only difference is that
the 'charting library' and the 'database' are probably accessed from
Javascript in a web page rather than, say, BASIC. Javascript was
developed by Netscape for exactly this purpose.

I did some work with Google maps a few years ago (2008), putting GPS
readings onto a map. Trucks were sending out GPS positions over SMS
that were being stored in a database so we should have been able to
draw a map of the route they had taken. Unfortunately the positions
were only every 10 minutes so it wasn't useful enough to implement.

Demo sample: http://Azonic.co.nz/maps/map.html



* The first edition of 'The Road Ahead' did not mention the internet
at all, it was later added. With Windows 95 there was the original MSN
which was walled off from the internet and was for W95 users only. It
failed.

** Spyglass wrote IE on the basis of a royalty payment (of $5.00 I
think ?) for every copy sold. MS claimed that it never _sold_ any
because it gave them all away, so it never paid anything to Spyglass
who then went broke. In my view if it was sold as part of Windows then
that was a sale.
```
